```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> In addition to new methodologies which departed from the procedural model, 
> hardware was making even more dramatic leaps and bounds. Things got smaller, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> for, and is not good at, this.

The more things change, the more they stay the same.  Have you heard of 
"diskless workstations" and the Linux Terminal Server Project?  They're 
being used as dumb terminals (minimum system requirement - original 
Pentium 133, 32MB RAM, 2MB video memory).  They boot from the network, 
and they only thing they do is render video from X (GUI) sessions off 
the server.

I'm planning on trying a setup like this at my children's school, in 
their library.  Set 'em up with Firefox to search the library's 
catalog(ue), and *poof*!  Instead of 10 useless computers, they now have 
10 prime card catalog(ue) lookup stations!

http://www.ltsp.org is the website...  It makes a lot of sense.  Users 
can connect from any of the workstations, and they'll mount the same 
home directory - their files (and security profiles) follow them.

I'm sure I'll be even more fired up about it once I actually get it to 
work.  :)  (I'm also going to try to set something up like that here, so 
I can utilize the old computers I thought were just going to be tossed 
in the trash.)

Who'd have thought - a mainframe with GUI clients!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MF having issues?

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-03-04T02:50:37+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<46qvtgFc9rc9U1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1140796116.074700.17110@j33g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dtnp9a02ht9@news1.newsguy.com> <RJMLf.67898$H%4.24314@pd7tw2no> <469v39Fa4immU1@individual.net> <p1a802dpn9sut0uu4a1opqg619j3jdjkri@4ax.com> <1208nvo5pt6vac8@news.supernews.com> <l6o902l6tm4ujtr03fektdi84cu41i7ip9@4ax.com> <46mc3rFbb7s3U1@individual.net> <1141248351.375659.50400@p10g2000cwp.googlegroups.com> <46o0i6FbssfnU1@individual.net> <3533a$4407b31a$45491d7a$20406@KNOLOGY.NET>`

```

"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
news:3533a$4407b31a$45491d7a$20406@KNOLOGY.NET...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> In addition to new methodologies which departed from the procedural 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> "diskless workstations" and the Linux Terminal Server Project?

I know about diskless workstations and considered it for an application in 
England a couple of years back. I didn't know about the LTSP, but I have 
very little exposure to Linux.

 They're
> being used as dumb terminals (minimum system requirement - original 
> Pentium 133, 32MB RAM, 2MB video memory).  They boot from the network, and 
> they only thing they do is render video from X (GUI) sessions off the 
> server.
>

Hmmm... not 'thin', rather, 'emaciated' clients :-)

Distribution and Centralization have alternated in waves since the late 70s 
when it first became possible to distribute. I remember the Cyber 70 series 
which had separate processors to do its I/O so the CPU could concentrate on 
code execution. CDC were very proud of this 'distributed' architecture.

While it is certainly feasible to do this kind of thing, I believe that 
there is still a place for 'local intelligence' when it comes to utilizing 
bandwidth in the most effective way. Your diskless workstations will require 
constant refresh from the server (rather than caching it themselves) and 
all editing and validation will be done at the backend, which simply means 
error notifications and corrections must also be pushed down the line. A lot 
depends on what's driving it, the bandwidth, and the protocols. (Field by 
field, full screen, lots of different factors here that can influence 
performance.)

For simply serving up information, this could be a very good solution. I 
have some reservations where volume data entry is occurring, but I haven't 
looked inot deeply enough to have an opinion as yet.

I think there is a place for centralization for certain types of application 
(and yours may be one), but I don't see it as a general solution.

> I'm planning on trying a setup like this at my children's school, in their 
> library.  Set 'em up with Firefox to search the library's catalog(ue), and 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> the trash.)

It certainly sounds like fun. :-)

Good luck!

>
> Who'd have thought - a mainframe with GUI clients!
>
If it wasn't for that fat penguin,  I'd go and look at it :-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: MF having issues?

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2006-03-03T17:01:39-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1141434099.274392.40070@p10g2000cwp.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<46qvtgFc9rc9U1@individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1140796116.074700.17110@j33g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <dtnp9a02ht9@news1.newsguy.com> <RJMLf.67898$H%4.24314@pd7tw2no> <469v39Fa4immU1@individual.net> <p1a802dpn9sut0uu4a1opqg619j3jdjkri@4ax.com> <1208nvo5pt6vac8@news.supernews.com> <l6o902l6tm4ujtr03fektdi84cu41i7ip9@4ax.com> <46mc3rFbb7s3U1@individual.net> <1141248351.375659.50400@p10g2000cwp.googlegroups.com> <46o0i6FbssfnU1@individual.net> <3533a$4407b31a$45491d7a$20406@KNOLOGY.NET> <46qvtgFc9rc9U1@individual.net>`

```

Pete Dashwood wrote:
>  They're
> > being used as dumb terminals (minimum system requirement - original
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Hmmm... not 'thin', rather, 'emaciated' clients :-)

I use LTSP on my client's sites. The old PCs that would otherwise be
landfill with all the disks ripped out and a PXE ROM in the NIC
(translation: boots over network) makes a reliable terminal. It boots
over the net and then attaches using NFS back to the server. Logins use
the user's home directory on the server so logging in from anywhere
gives the same setup and file set for the user. The terminal only runs
the X server (plus it can load and run selected software as
appropriate), the applications run on the server (or another server)
and therefore use the server's disk and CPU power.

> While it is certainly feasible to do this kind of thing, I believe that
> there is still a place for 'local intelligence' when it comes to utilizing
> bandwidth in the most effective way. Your diskless workstations will require
> constant refresh from the server (rather than caching it themselves) and

If it was Windows then it would require 'constant refresh from the
server', but this is X and this was designed from the start to be a
multiuser/multiserver network protocol. So no, it does not require
'refresh' at all and does local caching and does use bandwidth
effectively.

> all editing and validation will be done at the backend, which simply means
> error notifications and corrections must also be pushed down the line. A lot
> depends on what's driving it, the bandwidth, and the protocols. (Field by
> field, full screen, lots of different factors here that can influence
> performance.)

<shrug> the server runs the database engine so a fat client will be
sucking database accesses over the network. Which uses more bandwith
depends on what gets sent over the network. X maintains the graphics
screen in the client so only 'api calls' go between the application and
the X server (the client machine GUI engine is the server for X, the
application program is the client).

> For simply serving up information, this could be a very good solution. I
> have some reservations where volume data entry is occurring, but I haven't
> looked inot deeply enough to have an opinion as yet.

Having the application on the same box as the isam files/database
engine is __FAR__ faster than shovelling file data over the network.
Don't mix this up with Windows TSE which sucks bandwidth big time.

> I think there is a place for centralization for certain types of application
> (and yours may be one), but I don't see it as a general solution.

There is usually some level of centralization, most often the minimum
is the database.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> "LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message 
> news:3533a$4407b31a$45491d7a$20406@KNOLOGY.NET...
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Hmmm... not 'thin', rather, 'emaciated' clients :-)

Yep - somewhere between "thin" and "dumb"...  (Can't think of a good 
word, but the name "Calista" keeps coming to mind...)

> While it is certainly feasible to do this kind of thing, I believe that 
> there is still a place for 'local intelligence' when it comes to utilizing 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> performance.)

I've yet to see it in action, but supposedly, this isn't the way it's 
designed.  The "X" server (the beefy Linux box) communicates with the 
"X" client (which runs in RAM on the workstation) - so, the only thing 
that would be passed across the network is the internal stuff you'd 
normally have between the OS and its GUI layer.  (Of course, streaming 
video is another matter... :>  But, some folks have run 40-50 clients on 
a 100-mbps network with no noticeable problems.

I guess that's the difference between it and something like VNC.  VNC is 
rendering an exact copy of something on another display.  The LTSP 
clients are actually rendering the GUI themselves - so, I imagine things 
like changing focus when you tab to a new field would be done by the 
client, not the server.  (Any onchange events, though, would fire on the 
server - that's the way it's supposed to work, though.)

> For simply serving up information, this could be a very good solution. I 
> have some reservations where volume data entry is occurring, but I haven't 
> looked inot deeply enough to have an opinion as yet.

My interest is in a library environment, where users would be able to 
browse the net, as well as do things like working on documents, from any 
of the workstations.  Administration would be a breeze, and the users 
could actually save files on the server, and get to them no matter where 
they logged on.

>> Who'd have thought - a mainframe with GUI clients!
>>
> If it wasn't for that fat penguin,  I'd go and look at it :-)

Tux being fat is a good thing!  It means that he's always at the 
keyboard, making things better!  ;)
```

```

LX-i wrote:
> I've yet to see it in action, but supposedly, this isn't the way it's
> designed.  The "X" server (the beefy Linux box) communicates with the
> "X" client (which runs in RAM on the workstation) -

In X terminology they are the other way around.  The X Server runs on
the client machine or terminal and controls the actual display, the X
Clients are the application programs that are displaying data and
getting input from the server.

This is reasonable as for each server (screen, keyboard, mouse) there
may be several client programs, which may be distributed around the
network.  For example I can start a program on my archive machine in
the garage and have it connect to my X Server running a KDE session
locally and it will open a window as if it were just another program.
```

```
Hmm, I'd guess we have maybe 1500 COBOL programs.  And I certainly wouldn't
consider replacing them with any packaged programs.  Nor would I rewrite
most of them just for the heck of it, even if we had an alternative to COBOL
(which we don't).  But if we did have an alternative to COBOL (and honestly,
I don't mean Java!) that was "better" than COBOL (better, of course, being
my opinion only), I would certainly love to see new development in this new
language, and perhaps some rewriting of old programs if necessary to
interface to these new programs.

But there certainly is the issue of not COBOL being a *bad* language, just
that it's not a *popular* one, at least for the "younger" generation (I'm
only 36, so...).  We had a heck of a time finding a single new COBOL
developer last year.  Not that there weren't a lot of applications (I'm not
sure), but there just weren't a lot of the kind of person we were looking
for.  Is that the fault of COBOL?  Maybe...

Personally, I'm just trying to "live" with COBOL.  Just today I was working
on taking Michael Wojcik's suggestion with regard to using some OO features
in non-OO COBOL.  So far it is looking promising; but it's only been one
day...

Frank
```

```
It might be, Mr Swarbrick, that - in the slang of those who used to 
consume tobacco-producte - if tailor-mades aren't available one might do

<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/6c29c3f3dc17173c?dmode=source&hl=en>

--begin quoted text:

Years ago the Wall Street Journal did a story on one of the major NY
houses... I think it was Morgan Stanley or Morgan Guaranty or the like. 
They hired *only* the 'unhireable'... kids with BAs in Library Science,
Art History, etc... they put these kids through two years of hell, 60 -
70 hr weeks, and turned them into *crackerjack* programmers... and then
saw said kids being hired away by the competition at double or triple
the salary.  When asked why a raise did not accompany the completion of
the course the HR representative replied 'Oh, we cannot do that... all
the money has been taken up by training.' (compare this with 'Oh, we
cannot upgrade the door-locks... all the money went into oil-paintings
to hang on the walls.')

--end quoted text

So... 'the kind of people' might be out there, but they might need stimuli 
like training and bonuses and being made to that they are important to the 
organisation... you know, stuff that often gets reserved for the Managers, 
not mere (sniff) technical folks.

In your defense, Mr Swarbrick, I recall that you work for a bank... and 
banks are, in my experience, *notoriously* foolish when it comes to such 
things.  I can't recall the details of the project but I remember reading 
a Computerworld article how a VP of DP at Mellon Bank in Pittsburgh wanted 
to use one of those newfangled IBM PC AT machines for something... and had 
to go through five other levels of management to get sign-off on a 
US$20,000 project... that wound up saving the office US$60,000 in its 
first year of implementation.

DD
```

```
Jeff,

> Java...  We're guessing that it's likely to have the longest
> "un-messed-about-with" lifespan, unlike C, C++, C#...

What's wrong about C ? I'm not telling since 1974 but at least from 1986 did 
you ever had any compatibility problems ?
Of course it's not the best language to write, C++ is much better and 
powerful especially if you initially design your app assuming C++.
Regarding Java, I'm in it since 1.0 and every time SUN is releasing a new 
version I'm a little bit f...d because of incompatibilities !
As of now (IMHO) the longest "unmessed" lifespan you can expect is in C++, 
especially if you can stick with GNU.

Regarding your decision to move out from Cobol - it's just the matter of 
size of your app.
If you can afford to rewrite (and redesign !!!) it from scratch, that I 
agree it's better to do that sooner than later.
If you stick like me with 3000 Cobol programs size of 1M source each, you 
will not go anywhere for a reasonable money.
It should be really good investments and reasons to do that.

The alternatives were already named - Fujitsu (but they have some Cobol 
issues with initializing, and thay don't have rs6000 and they are not going 
to support Win64 etc...), OpenCobol (if your Cobol app is not in 
ObjectCobol) - go ahead. Any platform is supported, etc.

So to move out from the Cobol it's a bussiness decision (IMHO) when you want 
to move to object model.
And in this case I agree that it does not make any sense to move to Object 
Cobol - it's better to move to any language which supports that initially, 
i.e. C++, Java, C#.

I might be wrong ether.
I remember in Moscow on 1985 (?) Usenix it was a lot of talks about ADA (as 
everybody knows Algol-68 went to nowhere and PL/I was a kind of a trash 
can). One smart professor (of course kidding) told that ADA was designed by 
US to push USSR programmers to accept this dead line and all US soon will 
switch to C++.
Every joke contains part of joke. Now I can say he was right (not about the 
reasons of creating ADA of course :-)))).

Regards,
Sergey
```

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>

```
[post re-ordered]

"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:ARvNf.83587$sa3.6227@pd7tw1no...
>
> Ok Oliver, as another Java user - want to put your two cents in about the 
> Java incompatibility issue ?

    You bet.

> Sergey Kashyrin wrote:

>> Regarding Java, I'm in it since 1.0 and every time SUN is releasing a new 
>> version I'm a little bit f...d because of incompatibilities !

    Not sure what you're refering to.

    The two biggest version compatibility issue I'm aware of is the 
introduction of the "assert" keyword in 1.4, and the "enum" keyword in 1.5. 
If you had a variable or method called "assert" or "enum" in a 1.3 program, 
you'd have to rename that variable or method to something else if you were 
to recompile with a new compiler. I've never named a variable or method 
"assert" or "enum", and in all the code I've maintained written by someone 
else, there was never a variable or method named "assert" or "enum", so I 
never had to worry about this problem. Note that Java is case sensitive, so 
a variable called "Enum" or "ENUM" would not need to be renamed.

    Other than that, all the other incompatibilities seem, to me, trivially 
minor. Sun has a list of them on their page, at 
http://java.sun.com/j2se/1.5.0/compatibility.html, and the compatibilities 
issues seem to almost always be related to source code depending on 
undocumented features (or depending on bugs in previous implementations). 
For example:

<quote>
Drag and Drop - Previously, the only drag and drop (DnD) protocol supported 
on X11 was the Motif DnD protocol. In 5.0, the XDND protocol is also 
supported, and the Motif DnD protocol has been reimplemented to not depend 
on the Motif library. It's possible that regressions might be caused by the 
difference between the new Motif DnD protocol implementation and one 
provided by the Motif library. However, since the Motif library's 
implementation is buggy, it's believed that the new implementation is at 
least as high in quality, as well as better supported.
</quote>

    This again, is something so obscure that I've never had to worry about, 
and never even knew about until I read it today to provide some references 
for this post.

Java 1.5 introduces 23 incompabilities (including the Drag and Drop, and 
"enum" keywords mentioned above), Java 1.4 introduced 7 incompabitilities, 
1.3 introduced 16, etc.

    As you can see, these numbers are relatively small, and include even the 
most (IMHO) insignificant incompability issues. I've personally never had a 
problem with forward or backwards compability with Java.

    - Oliver
```

```
Oliver,

>>> Regarding Java, I'm in it since 1.0 and every time SUN is releasing a 
>>> new version I'm a little bit f...d because of incompatibilities !
>
>    Not sure what you're refering to.

You gave your answer:

> Java 1.5 introduces 23 incompabilities (including the Drag and Drop, and 
> "enum" keywords mentioned above), Java 1.4 introduced 7 incompabitilities, 
> 1.3 introduced 16, etc.

That's more than enough to have a problems.
Of course all of that is not that bad if you dealing with server-side or 
independent application.

But if you writing an applet which suppose to work in any browser under any 
plugin, you'll get a huge headache.
1.0 -> 1.1 - did not work at all.
1.1 -> 1.2 - it was the most smooth transition. at least it was more 
differences (and adjustments) between MS JVM and SUN 1.1 and of course a lot 
of different bugs in each version of 1.1 (IBM 1.1.6, SUN1.1.7 with SYMC JIT, 
etc...)
1.2 -> 1.3 - a lot of security classes has changed their packages :-)) - you 
are pushed to dynamically load your JVM-specific wrappers :-))
1.3 -> 1.4 - default behavior of keyboard input has changed, so your 1.3 
code just works incorrectly in 1.4. You have to add additional call for EACH 
(!!!) visual component (each Panel etc). Of course you have to do that 
dynamically. Imagine the number of places in a program you have to 
correct...
etc.

After all of that SUN's "pure java certification" sounds like a big joke 
:-))

I'm not a Windows lover but .NET was initially done much better. Java 1.5 is 
just an attempt to catch the train. I think really .NET pushed developers of 
Java to do what they did in 1.5

Regards,
Sergey
```

```

"Sergey Kashyrin" <ska@resqnet.com> wrote in message 
news:VJFNf.49$kS2.132226@news.sisna.com...
> Oliver,
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> independent application.

    Well, the point of my post was to show you how few incompability issues 
there were (probably less than 50 over the entire life of Java!), how 
insiginificant these backwards incompatibility issues are e.g. a *POTENTIAL* 
change in the behaviour of Drag and Drop in one particular platform, X11.

    For that particular issue, maybe there is NO ISSUE AT ALL. But Sun 
listed it, because there is a RISK of a change in behaviour, because of a 
switch of implementation. That is how conservative Sun is being with their 
list, and is intended to show that for fair comparisons, 50 changes in Java 
may even be something of an over-estimation!

    Note also that these aren't only incompabitilies with the language 
itself, but with the class libraries as well. This may include, for example, 
GUI components (e.g. the Drag and Drop issue mentioned earlier).

    If you take both the C++ language, and its libraries for GUI, network, 
collections, encryption, remote method invocation, XML parsing, etc. I'm 
sure you'll find much more than 50 backwards incompatibility issues.

>
> But if you writing an applet which suppose to work in any browser under 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> etc.

    I'm not sure that it's "fair" to include MS JVM when talking about Java 
compatibility issues. There's an Java Language Specification published by 
Sun and freely avaiable (http://java.sun.com/docs/books/jls/ if anyone is 
interested), and I believe that MS JVM was violating parts of the 
specification. In that sense, what "MS JVM" supported is not actually 
"Java".

    So it would be like complaining that your C++ compiler has backwards 
compatibility issues, because it is unable to compile your Pascal source 
code.

    Other than that, I can't comment much on your complaints above, as I 
haven't done much applet development before 1.4. My experience lies mostly 
with applications, and there, as I've said, I've never had any version 
compatibility issue ever.

>
> After all of that SUN's "pure java certification" sounds like a big joke 
> :-))

    Sun's "pure Java certification" tries to draw a clear line around a 
blurry subject. I wrote about it a bit in another usenet post 
(http://groups.google.ca/group/comp.lang.java.programmer/msg/8c68c9b52f42df5e). 
Long story short, it was something Sun tried a while ago, but it didn't work 
out, so they're dropping it. But that is not nescessarily an argument that 
"developing in Java is frought with problems" or anything like that.

>
> I'm not a Windows lover but .NET was initially done much better. Java 1.5 
> is just an attempt to catch the train. I think really .NET pushed 
> developers of Java to do what they did in 1.5

    I'm not sure that .NET and Java were designed to solve the same set of 
problems. There are some things that .NET is better suited for than Java, 
and there are some things Java is better suited for than .NET.

    I think no one will argue that Java 1.5 borrowed a ideas from C# 1.0. 
But I also think C# 1.0 borrowed a lot of ideas from Java 1.4. And C# 2.0 is 
borrowing ideas from Java 1.5 too. Nothing wrong with that. Both 
languages/platforms are (overall) getting better and better.

    Also, I've heard a lot of complaints about .NET backwards compatibility 
issues as well. See http://www.gotdotnet.com/team/upgrade/apiChanges.aspx

    There are 24 "breaking" changes between 1.0 and 1.1, and 57 members 
which previously existed have now been removed. Apparently they don't count 
these removal of members as "breaking" changes; I can't imagine why not 
though -- wouldn't removing a member that your code relies on break your 
code?

    Between .NET 1.1 and 2.0, there are 130 "breaking" changes and 3610 
removed members!!!

    Sun's policy is mark discouraged members as "deprecated", but to leave 
them there so that old versions of software can still compile and function 
properly (though the compiler will generate warnings).

    - Oliver
```

```
On Thu, 02 Mar 2006 18:26:41 GMT, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
```

```
James,

I agree with your point that Java is (was) too young and timeframe of those 
changes are relatively short.
So you had to adjust your client app constantly, even for minor versions 
sometime.
I'm not against Java at all and as of now I'm using it about 50% of my time.

I just don't know what will happen next. So far for server applications all 
those incompatibilities were not serious issues.
More problems are on a client side where you (as a developer) can't control 
the version of Java, but of course any serious developers team can overcome 
that.
I expect in a next few years Java will be more stable and it will be less 
number of incompatibilities issues, so the idea to reuse somebody's 
component (or free component libraries) in Java is where the world is going.

Regarding .Net - I really don't have a "production" client app yet (I have 
it as a test in development in both J# and converting to C# using MS 
conversion assistant). And the main reason is just that nobody asked for it 
yet. I'm not for MS and I'm not against it. I just have to work with it 
because 99.99% of the "client side" are sitting on win2k/xp/etc. and from a 
business point of view you want to get advantage of that fact.
I'm in the position where I can't dictate the client what to use, so I have 
to support (i.e. app has to run) ANY possible platform.
Personally I prefer C# over Java but a little bit (I like operation overload 
in Algol-68, ADA and C++).

Speaking of Cobol, I'm not a Cobol programmer (of coures I know it but just 
to support a huge existing app) - I've started from Algol-60, PL/I, Fortran, 
old HP Basic and a lot of different assemblers, then mostly went to C/C++ 
(before java in 96).
The problem of Cobol is that you can't do some tasks efficiently (i.e. text 
processing etc) and I'm not sure that implementation of object model in 
Cobol gives you relatively readable code (probably it is for a Cobol 
programmer, but for me it's easier to think in terms of java or C++ classes)

Regards,
Sergey
```

```
Howard,

> As opposed to which language that does every task efficiently?

It's not only about languages of course.

Well-known phrase (I don't know the exact one - translating back to English 
from Russian :-)):
Programming of inefficient algorithms in assembler will not give you any 
sufficient advantage.

So, of course it's only "some tasks" which Cobol was not designed for.

For example I'ld like to see the Cobol source of dynamic hashtable or a 
relatively simple syntax parsing.
I know it is doable, but my feeling is that this code will be hard to read.

You can take a look at good samples in Fujitsu Cobol for .NET how to work 
with .NET objects.
After that (if you have to program for .NET) you will run from Cobol right 
away.

IMHO you can do everything (including Financial programming) efficiently in 
C++ , but you have to desing the system
initially thinking in it's terms.
i.e. when you create class "money" (which will include "currency") and 
introduce operations (+-*/) you shoud be very careful.
After that, the functional programmer will not care of possible glitches. It 
will be covered by this class...
C++ as well as ADA will not allow you to add velocity to the distance. It's 
all about quality of checking for possible bugs in a early stage.
Cobol never had that and none has been introduced so far for that in any 
Cobol-2010 ...

We went far away from "MF having issues" :-) But back to the topic, from my 
point of view they do have an issue.

Regards,
Sergey
```

```

Sergey Kashyrin wrote:
> Howard,
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Programming of inefficient algorithms in Assembler gives a significant
speed and size advantage over programming the same algorithm in Cobol,
or any of the other 3/4 GL languages. There are algorithms that could
not be programmed in Cobol (we had Assembler routines accessing VSAM
files in a manner which Cobol could not do).

> So, of course it's only "some tasks" which Cobol was not designed for.

Unlike Java? A language that has had functionality bolted on in so many
places that no one man can be deemed to be an expert in the entire
language?

>
> For example I'ld like to see the Cobol source of dynamic hashtable or a
> relatively simple syntax parsing.
> I know it is doable, but my feeling is that this code will be hard to read.

No. Easy to do and read. I have written a parser in Cobol and a
colleague wrote a hash table in Natural (similar to Cobol and which
could easily be translated into Cobol vitually verbatim). The code is
easy to understand.

>
> IMHO you can do everything (including Financial programming) efficiently in
> C++ ,

Except for ease of maintenance. A previously used quote:
<QUOTE>
It has been said of languages like C, C++, and Java that the only way
to modify legacy code is to rewrite it - write once and write once
again; or write once and throw away. On the other hand, it has been
said of COBOL that there actually is one original COBOL program, and it
only has been copied and modified millions of times.
- Wikipedia entry for Cobol.
</QUOTE>

> but you have to desing the system

A good design makes for a good start. (aye, and why am I beginning to
sound like DD?)

> It's
> all about quality of checking for possible bugs in a early stage.
> Cobol never had that and none has been introduced so far for that in any
> Cobol-2010 ...

Checking for possible bugs is something that can precede the design
stage (start with the project initiation document). Cobol has always
allowed that. I presume you are referring to data typing, something at
which Cobol does have some (but minimal) capability (try adding ABC to
123).

It would be a morceaux de gateaux to write C++ poorly. Cobol can also
be written and designed poorly. However, beautifully crafted functional
Cobol code is a joy to behold.
```

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** "charles hottel" <jghottel@yahoo.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<d14f7$4409e4b1$4f9c662$13111@DIALUPUSA.NET>`

```
I do not think that the issue is what COBOL can and cannot do.  As a 
language it can do most of what C,  C++, and Java can do. It does have some 
weakness in doing bit manipulations compared to those languages. Maybe the 
2002 COBOL standard addresses that, but having a standard is a far different 
thing from having a working compiler. However in the OO area there are 
differences between the various compilers and I think OO came late to COBOL. 
But I still do not think this is the main shortcoming of COBOL. All of the 
other languages have extensive standard libraries and COBOL does not.  Even 
C which is not OO has a standard library. I think the OO paradigm encourages 
and promotes the building of these libraries.  There is no inherent reason 
why COBOL could not have standard libraries that perform the same functions 
that other languages have, but for the most part it does not. On the IBM 
mainframe we have Language Environment but it does not provide things like 
TCP/IP or XML processing.

Well I am sure someone will chime in and say just call the other language's 
library functions from COBOL. Yes, but on the mainframe Java is done under 
Unix Systems Services so you have to know something about that as well as 
know about Java.  In the floating point area mainframe COBOL uses 
hexadecimal floating point and Java uses IEEE floating-point. With all the 
other differences and incompatibilities not yet mentioned, overall it is a 
steep learning curve that most people are unwilling to attempt. Mixed 
language applications are more complicated and harder to maintain. It is 
better if the standard library is in a language that you already understand 
and even better if it is OO so that you can override methods when you need 
to do so, and as Pete Dashwood would say even better if you can just cobble 
together components.

It seems the COBOL standards people would rather add XML verbs to COBOL 
syntax than add a standard XML library to COBOL.  With no standard library 
for the language as a whole we then get nonstandard, fragmented and vendor 
specific solutions/offerings. The Java community has a process for 
developing these libraries (JSR)and they are working together in that 
regard.  The COBOL community is not working together.

The power of the Java libraries is evident. Don Higgins has developed a 
shareware mainframe assembler project named Z390. He has done similar things 
before using Cobol and Assembler, but this time he is using Java and he told 
me how impressed he is with its power. He has programmed the same 
functionality in far fewer lines than before and I believe it is due to the 
power of the Java libraries.  Parsing is hard if everyone always reinvents 
the wheel but if you have a library which does regular expressions so 
everyone can do it then it is easier. The same is true of Collections.  How 
many times on this list has someone said that linked lists are hard or that 
they do not belong in a COBOL program, yet Java programmers use ArrayList 
and HashMap and other similar functions all of the time.


"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1141483031.975503.245880@e56g2000cwe.googlegroups.com...
>
> Sergey Kashyrin wrote:
…[70 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
"charles hottel" <jghottel@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:d14f7$4409e4b1$4f9c662$13111@DIALUPUSA.NET...
<snip>
>  ... In the floating point area mainframe COBOL uses hexadecimal floating 
> point and Java uses IEEE floating-point.

Just because this "topic" comes up not only here but in IBM-MAIN,  I did want to 
make certain anyone interested in mixing COBOL and Java on an IBM mainframe is 
aware that the COBOL / JVM run-time take care of AUTOMATIC conversion of 
floating point data in mixed language applications.

See (for example),
   http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igy3pg30/6.2.2.1

which says (in part),

"Java floating-point data is represented in IEEE floating point. Enterprise 
COBOL, however,
     uses hexadecimal floating-point representation. When you pass 
floating-point arguments by
     using an INVOKE statement or you receive floating-point data from a Java 
method, the
     arguments and data are automatically converted as needed. "
```

```
charles hottel wrote:
> I do not think that the issue is what COBOL can and cannot do.  As a 
> language it can do most of what C,  C++, and Java can do. It does have some 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> TCP/IP or XML processing.

I disagree.  I think the reason that there are no large public domain
libraries results directly from a combination of runtime fees and
inflated cost of the compilers. Cobol users, as a result, are nearly all
paid employees of large corporations, and few of them *own* the software
they write.  They are not *allowed* to contribute that code to common
libraries.

The business model used to sell the language directly opposes it's use
by ordinary programers for public domain libraries. Such things come,
for most languages, from the academic world, and as shareware. The
licence on on my compilers directly prohibits *giving* the software
away, or the public donation of *any* runable modules.

I do not mind contributing to the common weal, but damned if I am going
to court a lawsuit for the privilege.

Donald
```

```
"charles hottel" <jghottel@yahoo.com> wrote in message 
news:d14f7$4409e4b1$4f9c662$13111@DIALUPUSA.NET...

>language it can do most of what C,  C++, and Java can do. It does have some 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>COBOL does not.

Some flavours do have libraries. Fujitsu provides some very good OO 
libraries for COBOL. Of course, if you use them, your COBOL is not 
transportable... :-(

 But I don't disagree with your statement,, as a general rule.

> Even C which is not OO has a standard library. I think the OO paradigm 
> encourages and promotes the building of these libraries.  There is no 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> as well as know about Java.

I should think that by now people in mainframe environments are getting to 
grips with Java...?


> In the floating point area mainframe COBOL uses hexadecimal floating point 
> and Java uses IEEE floating-point.

Well, you got me there :-) I used floating point (COMP-1 and COMP-2) for 
many years on the mainframe and always considered it to be binary; not sure 
what you mean by Hex floating point. (Hex is simply a shorthand 
representation of binary...). On the PC I've used long and double types with 
Java and VB and never worried about what the underlying format is, other 
than "it's floating point...".  I'm not sure what the significance of your 
statement above is, Charles.


>With all the other differences and incompatibilities not yet mentioned, 
>overall it is a steep learning curve that most people are unwilling to 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

If you are going to use standard libraries the last thing you would do is 
maintain them. (If you do, they aren't standard anymore...) It comes back to 
my point about code not being maintained. So, the language the standard 
library is in, doesn't really matter. I  have used MFC from COBOL on the PC 
and didn't care that the functions are written in C or whatever... (I DID 
care that using this tied my application to the Wintel platform, and that is 
the main reason I don't do it anymore, but that is a separate issue...) 
Actually, whatever I do (in COBOL) ties my code to the Wintel platform, 
because that is what my development environment is targetting, so it isn't 
really a major issue :-) The only exception to this is if I develop an 
ActiveX or COM component which CAN be run on other platforms by a broker 
such as CORBA. This is one reason why I am so passionate about component 
solutions. You can run them anywhere; on the desktop or on a web page, 
locally or remotely.

> It seems the COBOL standards people would rather add XML verbs to COBOL 
> syntax than add a standard XML library to COBOL.  With no standard library 
> for the language as a whole we then get nonstandard, fragmented and vendor 
> specific solutions/offerings.

I agree 100%. I said as much here some time ago. It is as if people believe 
that adding "XML processing" verbs to COBOL will make it more attractive or 
desirable. It doesn't. I believe it has exactly the opposite effect, for 
precisely the reasons you described. We don't do it for SQL (and nobody 
thinks any the less of COBOL for that); why embed other functions into the 
langauge when they are best served by remaining outside it. (And this 
doesn't even begin to address the physical work for vendors in doing this, 
and whether or not it is so important that they can expect to sell more 
copies of the compiler if they undertake it.)


>The Java community has a process for developing these libraries (JSR)and 
>they are working together in that regard.  The COBOL community is not 
>working together.
>

I thought about this for quite a while. :-)   I have come to the conclusion 
that there is a different cultural "attitude"between these two communities. 
As my stock here is already pretty low, I won't elucidate :-) All I will say 
is that reading the Java forums and reading this one is an "interesting" 
exercise, not from the point of view of language content, but from the point 
of view of attitude, particularly with regard to "new" ideas... :-)


> The power of the Java libraries is evident. Don Higgins has developed a 
> shareware mainframe assembler project named Z390. He has done similar 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> expressions so everyone can do it then it is easier.

Speaking of regular expressions, I spent a delightful couple of days last 
week learning to decipher them and looking at open source from SourceForge 
for their Okapi offering.  I downloaded Rainbow and had hours of fun trying 
different regular expressions with it... During the course of this, I came 
to understand why people who don't know COBOL say it is lacking in string 
handling. They are used to creating a regular expression object and letting 
it do everything they want and then some.

I was stunned by how powerful these expressions are, once you get past the 
mysterious and arcane cloak they are shrouded with.  I needed something that 
would search free format text and find case insensitive matches on phrases 
and words, returning the context in which they occurred (from the start of 
the sentence they occured in, to the end of the sentence they occurred in.) 
Yes, you could write it in COBOL with several complex INSPECTS with POINTER, 
and you would need reference modification to pick up the context once you 
had identified it; say, 20 lines of  (fairly complex) code? Using a regular 
expression it is one line of code. (2 if you count instantiating the RegExp 
object.)  Granted, to the uninitiated it is an incomprehensible line of 
code, but once the scales fall from your eyes it is simply elegant. These 
expressions are currently being standardised by W3C for all languages and 
character sets and Okapi is already using them to do language translation.

VB provides a standard library for Regular expressions and so does Java. 
Even JavaScript and VBScript get to play in the same playground.

The kind of thing you can do is: " Tell me if there is a match for the 
phrase  "Show me the money!" (as written, with initial capital), but only if 
the name "Jerry Maquire" precedes it in the text, and only if  there is no 
four letter word that starts with "F" following it anywhere in the text." 
One line of code; a regular expression.

I think we should have a regular expression interface for COBOL. The power 
of this for string searching and matching is phenomenal.

As you mentioned, Charles, when it comes to parsing free format input, this 
is superb.


> The same is true of Collections.  How many times on this list has someone 
> said that linked lists are hard or that they do not belong in a COBOL 
> program, yet Java programmers use ArrayList and HashMap and other similar 
> functions all of the time.
>

Exactly. However they don't fit "What we do now..." :-)

Pete.

>
> "Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
…[75 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Java compatibility issues (WAS: MF having issues?)

- **From:** "charles hottel" <jghottel@yahoo.com>

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:46v3qnFckgovU1@individual.net...
>
> "charles hottel" <jghottel@yahoo.com> wrote in message

<snip>

> >> In the floating point area mainframe COBOL uses hexadecimal floating 
> >> point
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> of your statement above is, Charles.

Originally IBM mainframe hardware had its own floating-point format and it 
was called hexadecimal because the digits in the mantissa were hexadecimal 
digits. At first this seems like a strange choice because a normalized 
mantissa like .1xxxxxxxxxxx would actually have 3 leftmost leading zero 
bits. This seems wasteful of the hardware bits, but there are other 
redeeming properties that make it a good format overall.  Today's mainframe 
hardware continues to support the old hexadecimal format but also supports 
the IEEE format.  Some compilers now generate the IEEE instructions but 
IBM's COBOL does not.

See: http://www.research.ibm.com/journal/rd/435/schwarz.html  and

http://docs.sun.com/source/806-3568/ncg_goldberg.html for more information 
on this.

If you search the page at the last URL for "hexadecimal" you will find a 
short discussion about the tradeoffs of various floating-point formats. The 
IBM hexadecimal format is mentioned in that discussion.
```

```
> precisely the reasons you described.

As usual, I will limit my comments to one environment that I know about pretty 
well, that of IBM mainframes (z/OS particuarly),

There have been *many* MORE upgrades in recent years to "newer" releases and 
versions of IBM's "Enterprise COBOL" product specifically BECAUSE of there 
support for (not the way the Standard is goins)

   XML GENERATE
        and
   XML PARSE

(as well as the Enterprise COBOL V3.4 support for VERY large data structures 
primarily - not excluively - introduce to handle XML documents.

Adding "native language support" (in a phased manner) for IBM's Enterprise COBOL 
product *has* resulted in increased sales and upgrades as new features are 
added.  This certainly COULD have been done via IBM's OO support, or by 
intrinisic functions, or by Language Environemnt "callable services" (which I 
would maintain ARE a "library" of available routines for this environment). 
However, the native language support SEEMS quite popular in the environment 
where COBOL is still (most?) commonly used.
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:AwuOf.356734$6Q3.161745@fe07.news.easynews.com...
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
> news:46v3qnFckgovU1@individual.net...
…[34 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't disagree, Bill, but I believe there is a reason that it is....

If your fundamental perception is not "Let's use the right tools for the 
job", but rather: "I can do everything I want to do in COBOL"..."Ooops! 
COBOL can't do XML... Well, bloody well MAKE it because that's the tool I 
use!", then you'll get exactly this push from the User Base to get things 
made intrinisic to the language. The fact that this is simply unnecessary 
and only succeeds in bloating the language, is completely lost on the people 
who push for it. The vendor, wishing to sell more copies may well include 
it. In the short term it succeeds, but in the time it took to implement, the 
more astute and flexible people were moving to other tools where calling an 
XML parser is as natural as calling a print routine, or a standard function 
to let the User browse a directory.

Eventually, the ones who called for it to be part of the language fade away. 
In the meantime changes to the XML standards mean the vendor is locked into 
a cycle of keeping his COBOL compiler up to date with the moving target of 
the XML standard, for a user base that is diminishing anyway. If I was in 
the business of providing a COBOL compiler (and I am truly grateful that I 
am not) I would offer a simple interface to an external function for dealing 
with XML.

Do you seriously think that if IBM had implemented this through LE services, 
their User base would have been diminished?

I doubt it.

Pete.






>
> -- 
> Bill Klein
> wmklein <at> ix.netcom.com
>
```

```
Components. Bloody evangelist :-)

I was relating it as a comparison to what Howard was referring to. You 
could pass source for recompilation or give them a DLL - within a 
particular mainframe family *perhaps*.

Cast your mind back - softwaresimple - you made some reference to pic 
9(09) comp-5 in F/J. "Hmm, good idea", I thought for compatibility. I 
can do a global search on a project in N/E so changed all my pic x(4) 
comp-5 to yours when I found them. Recompiled - no big deal. Run the 
application - DISASTER ! The N/E support classes are all pic x(4) 
comp-5, so getting the results from them just wasn't kosher. Fortunately 
re-searching I CHANGED them all back again ! That's just one aspect.

If we respectively took the DLL approach - even there it wouldn't work 
smoothly would be my guess. And even with DLL you need the two runtimes 
and supporting DLLs (from either F/J or M/F classes). It's just a bloody 
mess.

Throw in Donald's reference to runtimes for good measure. Want to 
communicate between two COBOLs - Java, C-languages, whatever, and yes 
could use your component approach. The only satisfactory way being to 
avoid COBOL as much a possible. Next Question - why use COBOL ?

Jimmy
```

```
James J. Gavan wrote:
> Howard Brazee wrote:
> > On Sat, 4 Mar 2006 14:04:23 -0500, "charles hottel"

>

Not true. If you have signed across your intellectual property rights
then the product of your labour at your bosses' expense is his; if you
have not signed away your rights then the property remains yours
(certainly under UK law). I saw a software house fall foul of this when
they let go of a contractor who had designed and built the company
problem/change management system prior to making a major upgrade. Under
UK law they were not permitted to make any change to the code or the
system without his express written permission. It cost them a whole wad
of cash to buy the rights to the system.
```

```
No wonder lawyers make a nice living Alistair.

Back when I was studying for Chartered Institute of Secretaries. Common 
Law or Corporate Law - and I forget the descriptor - but if you are an 
employee and decide to go off into business on your own you are 
prohibited from seeking his customers, carbon-copying his products or 
services. (I think there was a time period).

Now whatever the specific rules are on the topic I'm referring to, what 
you've stated seems diametrically opposed, as a point of law. "The Law 
is an Ass".

Still another aspect of Law, and why my tutorials even covered it I have 
no idea. There was a 'British Citizenship Act' about 1947. If you were 
born in the Auld Sod - Ireland - prior to ....... 1920 (?????) then you 
were AUTOMATICALLY a British citizen, without any paperwork. (Bearing in 
mind births in Ireland were registered under British documentation, just 
as for you and me).

So my dear old Dad, born in Dublin in 1901, left Ireland at about the 
age of 14-16 with his family to settle in the Capital of Ireland - 
Liverpool. Even joined the army in WWI - but too young to go before it 
finished. So then in about 1970 wants to visit his second son, (not me), 
here in Calgary. The bullshit he had to go through to prove he was a 
Brit and get a UK passport. He would have had an easier time if he had 
been in the KGB !

Jimmy
```

```
> services. (I think there was a time period).

That is to some extent still the case. You can be prosecuted for
downloading the CRM (theft) and most places send you on gardening leave
the minute you resign. However, few people realise that legally the
employer can only enforce a small period of notice (one month if paid
monthly, one week if paid weekly) and if he tried to enforce the
contracted period of notice then a brief trip to court claiming
'restraint on trade' and 'unable to earn a living' will free the
employee.

>
> Now whatever the specific rules are on the topic I'm referring to, what
> you've stated seems diametrically opposed, as a point of law. "The Law
> is an Ass".

No. There are intellectual rights, copyright, publishing rights . . .
The fact that you get paid to do a job does not mean that the employer
has any rights to the code or design you created. Unless you sign those
rights away (I have done so once but then I didn't want the Inland
Revenue's Computerised Environment for Self-Assement code/design).

>

…[14 more quoted lines elided]…

KGB=FSB now.
```

```
Sergey Kashyrin wrote:
> James,
> 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> programmer, but for me it's easier to think in terms of java or C++ classes)

Sergey,

I can understand your comments about COBOL, and in the non-COBOL world 
you are not the FIRST with the same reaction. To us it seems SO EASY - 
but if it's not your language of choice then there is a learning curve.

I would however disagree with you about handling strings/text. Even 
solely within Procedural code there are a tremendous number of features 
for manipulating text; add to that what one can do with OO and it 
becomes even more flexible - at least in my view. I consider that just 
one feature in COBOL the EVALUATE is considerably more flexible than the 
traditional CASE statement in other languages.

Unfortunately the COBOL community hasn't picked up on OO COBOL, which 
makes the topic academic - and probably in the long term it will have no 
more usefulness than the COBOL language in total.

Jimmy
```

```
On Fri, 03 Mar 2006 20:05:39 GMT, "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
wrote:

>> It may be more flexible, but it is possibly the least intuitive
>> command in the language.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>REWRITE, SEARCH and SORT statements.


 Evaluate printSnapshot Also sendEmail                    
   When true also true                                    
     move both-snapshots-and-email       to retcode       
     move ' both snapshots and email'    to retcode-text  
   When true also false                                   
...

or
Evaluate TRUE Also TRUE                                       
    When printSnapshot also sendEmail                         
> :>>     move both-snapshots-and-email       to retcode      
> :>>     move ' both snapshots and email'    to retcode-text 
     When printSnapshot also NOTsendEmail                     
> :>>     move snapshots-only                 to retcode      
> :>>     move ' snapshots only          '    to retcode-text 
...

or
 move zero to truthValue                                            
 if C1 add 1 to truthValue end-if                                   
 if C2 add 2 to truthValue end-if                                   
 if C3 add 4 to truthValue end-if                                   
 if C4 add 8 to truthValue end-if                                   
 evaluate truthValue                                                
      when 0                                                        
             *> nothing is true...                                  
      when 1                                                        
             *> C1 ONLY is true                                     
      when 2                                                        
             *> C2 ONLY is true                                     
      when 3                                                        
             *> C1 AND C2 are true                                  
   Note: This would be the extent required for the example given.   
   same number of WHENs but ALSO is not required)...                
        when 4                                                      
               *> C3 ONLY is true                                   
        when 5                                                      
               *> C3 AND C1 are true                                
        when 6                                                      
               *> C3 AND C2 are true                                
        when 7                                                      
...
or
 EVALUATE HOLD-CAMPUS-CODE-PRT ALSO ST-EXT ALSO ST-INST        
     WHEN 'BDB1'                   ALSO SPACE  ALSO 'BD'       
     WHEN 'BDB1'                   ALSO 'L'    ALSO 'BD'       
     WHEN 'BDB2' THRU 'BDB3'       ALSO 'W'    ALSO 'BD'       
     WHEN 'BDB2' THRU 'BDB3'       ALSO 'O'    ALSO 'BD'       
     WHEN 'CSC1'                   ALSO SPACE  ALSO 'CS'       
     WHEN 'CSC1'                   ALSO 'P'    ALSO 'CS'       
     WHEN 'CSC2' THRU 'CSC3'       ALSO 'Z'    ALSO 'CS'       
     WHEN 'DND1' THRU 'DND2'       ALSO SPACE  ALSO 'DN'       
     WHEN 'DND1' THRU 'DND2'       ALSO 'N'    ALSO 'DN'       
     WHEN 'DND3'                   ALSO 'Y'    ALSO 'DN'       
     WHEN 'HSH1'                   ALSO SPACE  ALSO 'HS'       
     WHEN 'HSH1'                   ALSO 'B'    ALSO 'HS'       
     WHEN 'HSH1'                   ALSO 'M'    ALSO 'HS'       
     WHEN 'HSH2' THRU 'HSH3'       ALSO 'X'    ALSO 'HS'       
         CONTINUE                                              
     WHEN OTHER                                                
         MOVE ERR06       TO PL-ERROR                          
...
```

```
Oops ! Should have read your response first. In replying to Howard I

EVALUATE - my big favourite - beats nested IFs every time for me. I'll 
wager if I had to depend on the technical specification, I may never 
have used it. However Jerome Garfunkel (the singer's brother) in his 
COBOL 85 paperback illustrates it very nicely. I wouldn't claim I even 
use all the options, but again given an example, sure works fine for me.

Can't help you on MERGE, RELEASE, SORT - never used them. Well I have 
used SORT on one occasion but at its very simplest lowest level.

----------------------------------------------------------------
Surprised REWRITE was problematical for somebody as savvy as you.

set RecordFound to true

Read MyFile using My Key
      invalid key set RecordNotFound to true
End-Read

if RecordFound :-

We've found the record  (key)12345(Name) Joe Smith
we change it 		(key)12345(Name) Bill Smith

*** Note : Changing the PrimeKey is a No-No !

if (RecordFound) AND (RecordChanged)
      Rewrite MyRecord
End-if

What's causing you confusion Oliver ?
--------------------------------------------------

SEARCH - I never used to use it, then asked here. I don't know whether 
or not the topic was 'SEARCH' but go back through the archives, some 
years ago - you'll get lots of decent tips. Briefly, get your list in a 
meaningful ascending sequence, and indexing gives you very good performance.

Can't use it with OO COBOL for which you need to compare - bearing in 
mind it's only F/J and M/F that have self-contained OO support.

SortedCollection - querying your collection size you can do a binary 
chop to reduce the number of elements being compared

OrderedCollection (sequential) - really means going through all elements 
until you find the one you want - using a callback

Dictionary(Your Java Maps) - Invoke myCollection "includesKey" using 
thisKey returning Boolean

Jimmy
```

```
> SortedCollection - querying your collection size you can do a binary chop

Obviously collections are implemented differently by different vendors. I 
would not search the collection to get the size of it. The Collection object 
has the number of elements in the collection as a property. (That is using 
the Fujitsu NODE collection. So does VB) If I'm building my own collection, 
there is a factory global that has the number of elements and it is added to 
when an add method is invoked successfully and subtracted from when an item 
is removed succesfully.)

> OrderedCollection (sequential) - really means going through all elements

So, can you explain why you differentiate between a sorted collection and an 
ordered one? Do you mean the sorted one was created randomly then sorted? 
Was the ordered one created and maintained in sequence? When it comes to 
searching, there should be no difference, except that if the sorted one was 
created chronologically, then the item numbers for the elements would not 
necessarily be sequential after it was sorted, whereas with the ordered one 
they should be (we could expect the whole set to be renumbered  from the 
point of insertion if an item was inserted.)

Now THIS you could use a binary chop on... whether you searched by key or 
item number.

I think this is a MicroFocus term. I'm not familiar with it.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> "James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
> news:Am5Of.97656$B94.27605@pd7tw3no...
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Let's qualify that a bit and it may be the difference between F/J and 
M/F approaches.

 From a look at F/J examples only (not background detail), it appears 
using a separate Record Class, (which is they way I think you approach 
it personally anyway), the 'textual' information in the Record Class 
becomes the object element in the Collection.

M/F has two options - create a Collection of Values (that's your 
'textual') or create a Collection of References (objects - i.e. use the 
text to create an object element which is displayable in GUIs).

Obviously with of Values (text) you could implement a SEARCH (I think, 
never having tried it). However trying to search on a key in something 
which is in displayable format, e.g. '12345 Joey Brown' - without 
bringing that back to text, no immediate way of identifying '12345' or 
'Joey Brown' as the particular object has no recognition of the two 
fields, even though for display purposes you may have a Tab key in 
between. So with the latter use 'Compare' methods for equality or otherwise.

Does that make sense ?

> Not sure what you are getting at here, Jimmy.
> 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Terminology I suppose -  creation of, or changes to the collection are 
maintaining both 'size' and 'capacity' so these are two 'properties' 
being set. Capacity might be 100 elements, (your initial setting for the 
collection) whereas 'size' the number of elements you physically have 
could be 52.

>>OrderedCollection (sequential) - really means going through all elements
> 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Ordered Collections - two examples :-

( for the sake of 2Jo in that thread "ouvrir ....." reference MS Word, I 
included Class MakeAstring). Taking the input string sent to MakeAstring 
the resulting collection from CharacterArray is in fact an Ordered 
Collection, just dropping the intervening white spaces.

Cast your mind back to Dialog System - I bet the feature existed when 
you were using it. DS uses Field Numbers for each control/(component ?) 
in ascending sequence - don't know DS but they have a standard feature 
'DataSet', which you might vaguely recall.

Independently, unknowingly, I arrived at the same design pattern using a 
Dialog Template Class. So as I pass parameters(properties) for the 
control to the Template I 'add' to two Ordered Collections, (no 
positioning by index, it's the next increment of the index). The first 
collection holds the 'displayable object' and the second, together with 
other information, the literal for the Method-name to be returned to 
after receiving a Windows Event. These two are created in sync, so no 
confusion. I can use the Field Number (index) to retrieve from either 
collection as I wish.

Now obviously there are lots of other things you can do with Ordered - 
but that's been my use to date, plus when dabbling with Treeviews to get 
Level information, the M/F support methods will return Ordered 
Collections containing elements immediately subordinate to the Level 
'Label' you are querying. (Treeviews and Master Menus, with dropdowns 
really *are* using the Ordered Collection concept).

Sorted Collections -

Entering random lines keyed in by, say a customer, for a Purchase Order, 
you could start with an Ordered Collection and convert it to Sorted, 
after the event. However, my approach has been to use Sorted from 
scratch - the Default Sort being ascending from left to right. I use 
this primarily for Combo Box droplists and Listboxes, ensuring the 
'displayables' appear in a meaningful sequence. By sub-classing you can 
override the Default Sort to create a new sort key.

> Now THIS you could use a binary chop on... whether you searched by key or 
> item number.
>
My reference to binary chop - something I twigged from what you had 
written - see below about Searching.

>>until you find the one you want - using a callback
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

I can't recall the Fujitsu syntax without looking it up. But both F/J 
and M/F have what I call a 'dual approach' using dictionaries. In the 
case of M/F check the dictionary with "includesKey" returning a Boolean.
Having gotten a True or False, then a second invoke to retrieve the 
element you are after. Daft approach for both - why not one single 
invoke returning the Boolean and 'ifTrue' return the element as well !

Not exactly above, but Searching.

First - somebody from Brazil, "How can I implement as in on-line help 
files, a few keystrokes and go to the element I want ?". It was 
suggested he use Activex. Having done a fair bit of support classing I 
understood what few extras were necessary. So having the classes 
available, just added. The Dialog Template already had "wantAllKeys", 
that is, capturing all keystrokes. The entry-field was given the 
property, 'Return Keystroke immediately'.

The Search Dialog (1) an entry-field and (2) a Listbox containing a 
variety of cheeses in ascending alphabetical. So you capture "G" then 
"Go" until perhaps you get to "Gouda", or even before the full word.
Return the keysroke events, let's say "Gou" - convert input and element, 
both to either Upper or Lowercase and compare input(1:3) to 
element(1:3), if a match return to Dialog and display (highlight)nearest 
element in the Listbox.

Works great - and not difficult to implement with all that support stuff 
already written. Haven't volume tested, but may work quite well on 2,000 
elements - just don't know. I did suggest that to reduce the 
comparisons, use the binary-chop technique to reduce the search area.

Not related to above - but somebody from Sweden said he had tried 
'Searching' using similar and it was a dead loss on performance. Out of 
interest I queried why. "Well", he wrote "There are 10,000 Anderssens in 
my lists, so I've had to take a different approach using the mailing 
address as well".

Hmmmm. I see his point. Then I check the Calgary telephone directory. I 
think (1) Vancouver, (2) Toronto and then us third, for largest Chinese 
community in Canada. Chinese surname LEE in Calgary - at least 1,000, if 
not more; and just to make life easy there are 28 entries 'Lee, T'.

Design concepts are fine - but have to be adjust to your localised 
problem :-).

I enjoy Collections more than anything in OO - so I'll miss the fun 
dropping COBOL.

Jimmy
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:


Let's take it out of sequence for a minute. I wrote...

 >>Obviously with of Values (text) you could implement a SEARCH (I think,
 >>never having tried it).

Thought about that. There's no bloody way I could do that with the 
Procedural SEARCH syntax against an OO Collection. So was going to ask 
you how the hell you do it ! Glad we've cleared that one up, and you 
latched on to what I'm a actually doing.

> 
> Yes, I understand what you are saying. You are talking about using the 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> dynamic tables anyway (without help from oputside sources and environments).

Some day when King Arthur returns to this mortal coil, somebody *might* 
implement J4's Dynamic Working-Storage Tables.
> 
> Diiferent contexts of the word SEARCH. It's clear now.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> them.

I think Will mentioned it in his book, (yours now being dog-eared or 
lost), but it is a feature M/F picked up from Smalltalk when designing 
Collections. You have the ability to 'grow' automatically as you write 
above. However an example from Adele Goldberg in her 'Smalltalk 80'. 
Small ferry boat shunting cars across a harbour, (perhaps Seattle). The 
boat has a max CAPACITY of 6 vehicles. So if you want to automate it you 
can load each vehicle automatically giving you SIZE and check CAPACITY 
so that you don't push car #1 off the front as you go to load car # 7 !

Probably a more effective usage, rather than just growing, element by 
element, which means you are repetitively going though an 'expansion' 
procedure. Knowing that you have a list which could contain 100 
elements, you can start by making the collection have a CAPACITY of 100. 
  You only load 50 elements. invoke myCollection "size" returning size. 
If SIZE is less than CAPACITY - stupid method-name coming up - invoke 
MyCollection "growTo" using mySize - "growTo" meaning increase or 
decrease - adjusting your memory requirements.

> Yes, I understand that, but the colllection classes I use in FJ and VB 
> simply don't require capacity. (Neither should they, in my opinion.)

Nor do they in M/F - just a feature you can use if you want to - I don't.
> 
>>>So, can you explain why you differentiate between a sorted collection and 
>>>an ordered one? 

I think we've cleared that one up too. Subsequently remembered you don't 
have a Sorted Collection class in F/J. Surprise, surprise not did J4 on 
first run around with Collections as the author was referencing F/J 
concepts. Same author in private e-mail queried the validity of 
Dictionary/Maps - in all innocence I posted my rely to him via 
softwaresimple.com., thinking others of you could comment. He didn't 
like that - back came a snotty reply and we haven't communicated since. 
(Politics, bloody politics !).

J4 only added it as a result of my bitching. It was Karl Kistler 
(Fujitsu-Siemens) who gave me the heads-up it had been added.

> 
>>Independently, unknowingly, I arrived at the same design pattern using a 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> object. 

So WOULD I ! M/F have support classes to create various collections but 
never figured out how you could do it so that one element could contain 
both the displayable object and the text for the method-name. Perhaps a 
two level collection - but couldn't figure it ? So while I consider it 
clumsy, it works.

Incidentally when defining dictionaries in M/F they can be expressed as 
follows :-

        Key     Data
        ----    ----
        Object  Object
        Object  Text
        Text    Object
        Text    Text

Given that you have a Primekey/DB-ID of 12345 and name Joe Blow each can 
be the Key or Data. With those options above you have to specify 
Dictionary structures - that's what I would have liked to have done 
rather than those two Collections I have above. (I wouldn't mind betting 
Robert Wagner could have figured this one for me).

The collection for the dropdown or list box could be a text file (it
> doesn't HAVE to be a collection), which could be maintained separately from 
> the control and even built dynamically by other code, before the control is 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> you simply don't add a horizontal scroll to the control).

We aren't poles apart on this one - briefly I'll try and describe :-

                       ----------
                       DBI Main
                       ----------

        -------------  -----------  ------------------
        EditCustomers  CustomerDBI  COBOLFile/SQLtable
        -------------  -----------  ------------------

                       -----------  ------------------
                       Customer      Tabbed Dialog
                       DialogParams
                       ------------ ------------------

EditCustomers is selected from the Master Menu and is the Business class 
for this sub-module. I have set up the info from Dialog Editor (not 
Dialog System) as a set of parameters/properties for each Control. These 
parameters are passed to TabbedDialog to create an instance which is 
CustomerDialog - don't need any more, so I can destroy/finalize 
CusomerDialogParams.

DBI - terminology I picked up from Will. DBI = Database Interface. Not 
mentioned in his book, but the white paper he sent me with his First 
Edition.

COBOL File/SQL Table - doesn't matter I keep them vanilla-flavoured so 
that I can access as necessary - directly from an invoke from Customer 
Edit or via the CustomerDBI.

Step 1 - all specific DBIs inherit from DBIMain.

Step 2 - Create a collection/dictionary - from EditCustomers invoke 
CustomerDBI using parameters - Step 3 below

Step 3 - rather than remember the syntax for creating collections I pass 
parameters to DBI Main to create any collection/dictionary. The 
parameters also include the positioning within an element for the 
columns, even though as a standard I allow three sequences identified by 
Level 88's - ByName (default), ByKey, ByOther if relevant.

Step 4 - Now I want to create the collection (independently of the GUI 
procedure). CustomerDBI now invokes "readNext" against a COBOL file or 
CURSOR SELECT for SQL. The object reference and method-name within 
Customer DBI are passed to File/Table as appropriate - in the case of 
SQL CURSOR - until reaching 'No more Rows' the Table class sends the 
Table row data, as required, back to CustomerDBI. The latter formats the 
text, the latter part of Step 3 - it now uses super DBIMain to add to 
the appropriate collection.

Step 5 - when the collection is complete it is now returned to 
CustomersEdit - which knowing the appropriate Field Number associated 
with a ListBox or Droplist now invokes the instance of CustomerDialog :

invoke os-CustomerDialog "setContents" using Field Number

(which is the INDEX position) of the appropriate displayable control in 
that First DisplayableObjectCollectioin above.

Step 6 - just like you I'm using the selection event to get the index 
position for the Listbox or Droplist which is returned to Controller - 
CustomersEdit. That now invokes CustomerDBI, by-passing it and checking 
  (super) DBIMain. I have a whole daffy of methods in DBIMain which 
'translate' or 'search' as necessary. The instance relates to only one 
original 'list' and I have the column positioning in the element to help 
me track.

Note - the empahsis above is to make screening a dumb terminal - no 
decision making other than Windows events. Creation/changing of 
collections is done entirely outside of GUI-ing. Back to your Compuserve 
mention of Three-Tier Systems - if I've misinterpreted - then it's your 
fault :-)

> So why not have one collection?
Got your answer above on that one.


> Yes, I use a special Fujitsu Collection for TreeViews called a Node. The 
> node object has properties and methids that allow icons and subclasses to be 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> http://ar.geocities.com/pcobolar/tutorial1/tvtutorial.doc)

That routine I described above is also used identically to access 
Files/Tables to create Treeviews - my test example had three levels :-

Level 0 = "Cheese"
Level 1 = Continent
Level 2 = Country
Level 3 = 12345 Cheddar Cheese.

> 
> This is good stuff but it is simply re-inventing the wheel. There are any 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> time you need a combo box selection...

Well hopefully above demonstrates that I am R-E-U-S-I-N-G. The code 
contained in the individual DBIs is minimal - the donkey work being done 
in DBIMain for ALL lists, whether displayable or not.

Checking Keys :

I sort of get the feeling you half mentioned it above. Retrieving the 
COBOL Primekey/or SQL ID. I asked in the M/F Forum, not really receiving 
a 'positive' which led me to,'This is the way to go'. Simon jumped in 
and suggested a parallel collection containing keys. I checked the 
Microsoft site - certainly getting the feeling that they were 
recommending the same approach. Just like you observed above, "Why two 
collections ?" - to which the answer is I wouldn't if I could have 
figured it out :-).

I have my PrimeKey/SQL-ID built into my list elements. And if I use the 
feature display ByKey - that's the sequencing I get. In the case of 
Droplists I don't want to confuse users with mnemonics when selecting 
'Canada' - tip from Ken Mullins, make the droplist Mono spaced font 
which stretches it out, exceeding the width of your droplist, so that 
the code is hidden to the right.

Jimmy
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:n6qPf.119714$sa3.33638@pd7tw1no...
>
> Probably a more effective usage, rather than just growing, element by 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> your memory requirements.

"ArrayList" is one particular type of collection available in Java. The 
documentation for using it is available at 
http://java.sun.com/j2se/1.5.0/docs/api/java/util/ArrayList.html

The way it works is when you create a new ArrayList, you can specify its 
initial capacity. If you don't, it just uses some arbitrary non-documented 
value (e.g. perhaps 10). If the size of the collection is the same as it's 
capacity, and you try to add another item, the collection will silently grow 
itself and, now having sufficient capacity, accept your item, all under the 
cover without you being aware that anything peculiar has happened.

There's an "ensureCapacity" method which you provide an integer representing 
the desired capacity. When you call this, the ArrayList will grow if the 
requested capacity is larger than the actual one, or simply ignore your 
message if the actual capacity is already larger than the requested one. 
There's no direct way of finding out what the actual capacity is (you could 
find out by using a debugger, for example).

There's also a "trimToSize" method which takes no arguments or parameters at 
all. The intended usage is for when you've finished changing the size of the 
collection (i.e. not adding or removing items anymore), you can then 
instruct the ArrayList to size itself so that its capacity exactly matches 
its current size to save a bit of memory.

>
> So WOULD I ! M/F have support classes to create various collections but 
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> Wagner could have figured this one for me).

    Not sure if I understood the question, but if you want a dictionary and 
you want to associate a key with two objects (and always exactly two objects 
for every single key), you could create a so-called "Pair" class, pack both 
your objects into the pair, then put the pair into the dictionary.

class Pair {
  DisplayableObject displayable;
  TextForMethodName text;
}

Pair p = new Pair();
p.displayable = myDisplayableObject;
p.text = "Object Label";

dictionary.put("key", p);

Pair anotherPair = dictionary.get("anotherKey");
print(anotherPair.text);

>
> I have my PrimeKey/SQL-ID built into my list elements. And if I use the 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> is hidden to the right.

    I don't know if the library you're using supports this, but droplists in 
Java can have an "ListCellRenderer" object associated with them. The 
ListCellRenderer object takes the actual content of the list, and takes care 
of their appearances on the GUI.

    For example, maybe the actual contents of your list (i.e. in memory) are 
a bunch of text strings like "http://myserver/images/image1.jpg", 
"http://myserver/images/image2.jpg", etc.

    And the ListCellRenderer doesn't merely display the text, but rather 
treats the text as an URL, connects to the server, downloads the file, 
treats it as an image, and displays it as one of the items in the drop list. 
(Not sure if you've ever seen a droplist with images in them; if not, you 
can see what it looks like at 
http://java.sun.com/docs/books/tutorial/uiswing/components/combobox.html#renderer )

    If your library supports something like a custom renderer, then you 
could probably go that route, and just only render the text that you want to 
be actually visible. If not, maybe you can take this idea and modify it to 
fit with the API of your library.

    - Oliver
```
